```mermaid
    Normal --> Normal: disconnect (count < threshold)
    Normal --> HoldDown: disconnect (count >= threshold within window)
    HoldDown --> HoldDown: reconnect → Penalized flag, announcement suppressed
    HoldDown --> Normal: holdDownDuration expires + flapWindow clears
```
*Diagram — Peer flap detection state machine*

**Outgoing DM delivery decision.** When `storeIncomingMessage` routes a DM, `TableRouter.Route()` returns a `RoutingDecision` containing `RelayNextHop` (identity), `RelayNextHopAddress` (validated transport address or `"inbound:"` key), and `RelayNextHopHops` (hop count from the route). The send path (`sendTableDirectedRelay`) uses the pre-validated address directly — no re-resolution — to avoid selecting a different session for the same identity with weaker capabilities. If the validated address is empty (retry path), re-resolution uses `resolveRouteNextHopAddress` with the original hop role: destination (hops=1) requires only `mesh_relay_v1`; transit (hops>1) requires both `mesh_relay_v1` and `mesh_routing_v1`. If re-resolution or send fails, gossip fallback triggers. Both outbound sessions and inbound-only connections are usable as relay next-hops. Relay state persistence (`relayForwardState`) is checked on both outbound and inbound paths — if the store is rejected (duplicate message ID or capacity limit), the send is treated as a failure (returns false) and a warning is logged (`relay_state_store_failed_outbound` / `relay_state_store_failed_inbound`). This ensures the caller sees the delivery as failed and can fall back to gossip rather than silently losing hop_ack and receipt routing context.

```mermaid
flowchart TD
    A[storeIncomingMessage] --> B[router.Route envelope]
    B --> C{RelayNextHop set?}
    C -- Yes --> D["sendTableDirectedRelay(identity, validatedAddress)"]
    D --> E{Send to validatedAddress ok?}
    E -- Yes --> Z[sent]
    E -- No --> G[tryRelayToCapableFullNodes gossip fallback]
    C -- No --> G
    B --> H[executeGossipTargets always runs in parallel]
```
*Diagram — Outgoing DM delivery decision: table route vs gossip*

**Intermediate relay hop delivery decision.** When `handleRelayMessage` processes a transit relay, it tries three strategies in order: direct peer (recipient locally connected), routing table lookup (`tryForwardViaRoutingTable`), and blind gossip (`relayViaGossip`). If all fail, the message is stored locally and gossip handles delivery. When `tryForwardViaRoutingTable` selects a route, it returns a `tableForwardResult` containing both the transport address and the route's synthesised `Origin` field (post-Phase-1 always `localOrigin` or, in fallback, the destination Identity — `Origin` was dropped from the storage dedup key). The `RouteOrigin` is persisted in `relayForwardState` for caller-stability only — `confirmRouteViaHopAck` at this intermediate node IGNORES the field post-Phase-1 and scopes hop_ack confirmation to the `(Identity, NextHop)` pair.

```mermaid
flowchart TD
    A[handleRelayMessage] --> B{Recipient directly connected?}
    B -- Yes --> C[tryForwardToDirectPeer]
    C --> D{Enqueue ok?}
    D -- Yes --> Z[forwarded]
    D -- No --> E{Routing table has route?}
    B -- No --> E
    E -- Yes --> F[tryForwardViaRoutingTable]
    F --> G{Enqueue ok?}
    G -- Yes --> Z
    G -- No --> H[relayViaGossip blind]
    E -- No --> H
    H --> I{Any peer accepted?}
    I -- Yes --> Z
    I -- No --> J[deliverRelayedMessage store locally]
```
*Diagram — Intermediate relay hop: direct peer → table route → gossip*

**Gossip fallback contract (release-blocking).** `TableRouter` in `node/table_router.go` implements the `Router` interface. It consults the routing table for a directed next-hop; if no usable route or session exists, delivery degrades to gossip transparently. Fallback triggers: `Lookup()` returns empty, `RelayNextHop` identity has no active session, `RelayNextHop` peer lacks required capabilities (relay-only for destinations, both relay+routing for transit hops). `GossipTargets` are always populated in every `RoutingDecision`. The same fallback applies at every level: origin node (`sendTableDirectedRelay`), intermediate hop (`tryForwardViaRoutingTable`), and `TableRouter.Route()`.

**Gossip target selection: session + pending peer merge.** `routingTargetsFiltered` in `node/routing_relay.go` builds the gossip target list from two sources: (1) active outbound sessions with healthy connections (scored by health, capped at 3), and (2) known peers without active sessions that can be reached via the pending queue (`queuePeerFrame` → `flushPendingPeerFrames` on session establishment). When scored session targets exist, remaining fanout slots (up to 3 total) are filled with non-session peers. When no sessions exist at all (bootstrap), all matching peers are returned uncapped. **Fallback-port deduplication:** when a session connects via a fallback port (e.g. `host:64646` instead of canonical `host:7777`), both the session key and the canonical address (resolved via `dialOrigin`) are marked in `sessionSeen`, preventing the same physical host from appearing both as an active session target and as a pending peer — which would waste a fanout slot on a duplicate. This merge prevents a timing hole where a message arriving before all sessions are established gets gossipped only to the sender (the only active session) and never reaches forward peers — `seen[ID]` dedup then permanently blocks re-gossip on subsequent attempts. Non-session peers receive their frames via `sendMessageToPeer`, which attempts delivery in three tiers: (1) outbound session via `enqueuePeerFrame`; (2) direct write to an authenticated inbound connection via `inboundConnForAddressLocked` — this covers the common case where the peer has connected to us (inbound) but we have no outbound session (CM slot full); (3) `queuePeerFrame`, which buffers the frame in the pending queue for deferred delivery. The inbound direct path (`mode="inbound_direct"` in gossip_message_attempt log) is the primary fix for the timing hole where a peer's inbound connection is already authenticated before the gossip frame arrives — writing directly avoids the queue entirely. Three drain triggers exist for already-queued frames: (1) outbound session establishment calls `flushPendingPeerFrames`, which sends all pending frames via the session's `sendCh`; (2) inbound connection authentication calls `flushPendingFireAndForget` in `trackInboundConnect`, which writes fire-and-forget frames (`push_message`, `push_notice`) directly to the inbound TCP connection; (3) route events trigger `drainPendingForIdentities` for `send_message` frames. The inbound drain in `trackInboundConnect` handles the reverse timing: frame queued before inbound connection authenticates. Only fire-and-forget frames are safe to write on the inbound conn because they don't expect a response that would interleave with the peer's request/reply dispatch loop. Request/reply frames (send_message, relay_message) must wait for an outbound session.

**Event-driven pending queue drain.** When a route to a destination identity becomes usable — either a direct peer connecting (`servePeerSession` startup), a transit route learned via `announce_routes` (`handleAnnounceRoutes`), a reconnected peer re-announcing the same unchanged routing table (`handleAnnounceRoutes` with `RouteUnchanged` status), a route withdrawal that exposes a surviving backup route (`WithdrawRoute` followed by a `Lookup` check), a TTL expiry that removes a higher-priority route and exposes a surviving backup (`TickTTL` returning exposed identities in `routingTableTTLLoop`), or a local peer disconnect that invalidates routes through the departing next-hop but exposes a backup via a different next-hop (`RemoveDirectPeer` / `InvalidateTransitRoutes` returning `ExposedBackups` in `onPeerSessionClosed`) — the node immediately scans its pending queue for own outbound `send_message` frames targeting that identity and attempts to deliver them via the newly available route. This is an event-driven optimization that eliminates two latency sources: waiting for the original target peer to reconnect (`flushPendingPeerFrames`), and waiting for the 2-second relay retry loop to fire. The drain goroutine is launched from `servePeerSession` (not `onPeerSessionEstablished`) to ensure `inboxCh` is actively being read — launching it earlier creates `s.mu.Lock()` contention that delays session establishment and can cause `inboxCh` overflow under concurrent load. Only `send_message` is drained — other frame types are not route-recoverable: `relay_message` and `announce_peer` are other peers' traffic handled by the relay retry loop and peer session flush; `relay_delivery_receipt` is delivered via the `relayStates` hop chain (`lookupReceiptForwardTo`), not via the routing table, so a new route to the receipt's Recipient does not create a delivery path for it. The `drainPendingForIdentities` function in `node/routing_drain.go` uses an extract-attempt-return pattern: matching frames are atomically removed from the pending queue under the lock (preventing concurrent drain goroutines from processing the same frame twice), delivery is attempted outside the lock via `sendRelayToAddress` (which returns a definitive success/failure signal), and frames that fail delivery are merged back into their original positions in the pending queue using index-based reconstruction — the extraction phase records each frame's `origIdx` and per-address metadata (`origLen`, `extractedPos`), and the return phase walks through the original position range interleaving kept and returning frames so that `flushPendingPeerFrames` — which delivers in slice order — maintains exact DM delivery ordering across recipients sharing the same peer address after route churn. Frames added by other goroutines during the unlocked delivery window are appended at the end. The retry counter is only incremented when a real delivery attempt was made (route existed, address resolved, `sendRelayToAddress` was called) — when no usable route exists (`RelayNextHop == nil` or address unresolvable), the frame is returned to the pending queue without burning retry budget and without any outbound state transition — the `Status` stays `"queued"` and `LastAttemptAt` is not updated, preserving the semantic that `"retrying"` in outbound state means a real network handoff was attempted. The frame waits for future drain cycles triggered by subsequent routing events. When `maxPendingFrameRetries` is reached after real failures, the frame is marked terminal instead of being returned — matching the retry contract of `flushPendingPeerFrames`. Expired frames are detected and marked terminal during the scan. All outbound state changes (terminal, retrying, cleared) are collected as deferred actions during the delivery loop and applied under the lock together with the pending queue return as one atomic in-memory update — so a concurrent reader never observes outbound state mutated while the extracted frames are absent from `s.pending`. (Queue-state is **in-memory only** now — disk persistence was removed — so there is no on-disk write to batch and no crash recovery here: a crash/restart loses pending state and the sender retries end-to-end. The batching is purely about cross-goroutine consistency.) When `handleAnnounceRoutes` processes a single announce, both accepted (new/improved) and unchanged (reconfirmed alive) identities are batched into a single drain set. Rejected routes (stale SeqNo, tombstone-blocked) do not participate in drain. The `announce_routes_processed` log emits three counters — `accepted`, `unchanged`, `rejected` — so operators can distinguish new routing events from benign reconfirmations. This three-state classification uses `RouteUpdateStatus` (`RouteAccepted`, `RouteUnchanged`, `RouteRejected`) returned by `UpdateRoute`.

```mermaid
flowchart TD
    A[Route event: identity reachable] --> B{Event source}
    B -- "servePeerSession startup<br/>(direct peer connected)" --> C[Trigger drain]
    B -- "handleAnnounceRoutes<br/>(transit route learned or reconfirmed)" --> D[Collect drain identities]
    B -- "WithdrawRoute<br/>(backup route exposed)" --> W{"Lookup: backup exists?"}
    W -- Yes --> D
    W -- No --> Z[No drain needed]
    B -- "routingTableTTLLoop<br/>(TTL expiry exposes backup)" --> T{"TickTTL: exposed identities?"}
    T -- Yes --> D
    T -- No --> Z
    B -- "onPeerSessionClosed<br/>(local disconnect)" --> P2{"ExposedBackups?"}
    P2 -- Yes --> D
    P2 -- No --> Z
    D --> C
    C --> E["drainPendingForIdentities(identities)"]
    E --> F["Lock: extract send_message frames from s.pending"]
    F --> G{Frame expired?}
    G -- Yes --> H["deferred: markOutboundTerminalLocked"]
    G -- No --> I["router.Route(envelope)"]
    I --> J{RelayNextHop + address resolved?}
    J -- Yes --> K["sendRelayToAddress(address, envelope)"]
    K --> M{Delivery confirmed?}
    M -- Yes --> N["deferred: clearOutboundQueuedLocked"]
    M -- No --> P["Retries++ (real attempt)"]
    P --> PA{Retries >= max?}
    PA -- Yes --> Q["deferred: markOutboundTerminalLocked"]
    PA -- No --> R["deferred: markOutboundRetryingLocked + return to pending"]
    J -- "No (no route)" --> NR["Return to pending without incrementing Retries"]
    H & N & Q & R & NR --> S["Lock: merge failed at original positions + apply deferred → one atomic in-memory update"]
```
*Diagram — Event-driven pending queue drain on route discovery*

**Route confirmation via hop_ack.** When a `relay_hop_ack` with status "delivered" or "forwarded" is received, the routing table promotes the route through the ack sender to `source=hop_ack` via `confirmRouteViaHopAck`. The ack sender's transport address (`senderAddress` in `handleRelayHopAck`) is the provably correct next-hop — even under gossip fan-out where multiple peers received the relay_message but only one actually delivered it. The peer identity is resolved from the transport address and matched against routing table entries. For table-directed relays, the `RouteOrigin` stored in `relayForwardState` is still threaded into `confirmRouteViaHopAck` by signature but post-Phase-1 is IGNORED — storage is no longer keyed on Origin, and the confirmation matches the claim on `(Identity, NextHop)` only. The parameter is retained on the signature for caller-stability (the relay-side plumbing continues to plumb it through `tableForwardResult`) but does not influence promotion outcome. `RouteOrigin` is persisted at every hop in the relay chain: at the origin node (via `sendRelayMessageWithOrigin` / `sendRelayToAddress`), and at intermediate hops (via `tryForwardViaRoutingTable` returning `tableForwardResult` with `RouteOrigin`). For gossip-originated relays where no specific triple was chosen (`RouteOrigin` is empty), the first matching NextHop in Lookup order is promoted. Both outbound and inbound relay paths persist `relayForwardState`, ensuring behavioral equivalence regardless of connection direction.

**Announce_routes relay gate (routing-only peers).** A peer that negotiates `mesh_routing_v1` but not `mesh_relay_v1` can participate in the control plane (receive and send route announcements) but cannot carry data-plane traffic (`relay_message`). Accepting routes from such peers would create entries pointing to a next-hop that silently drops relayed messages. To prevent this control-plane/data-plane mismatch, both inbound and outbound `announce_routes` dispatch paths enforce a relay gate: inbound frames from connections without `mesh_relay_v1` are silently rejected (`connHasCapability(conn, capMeshRelayV1)` in the `announce_routes` handler), and the outbound announce loop skips sessions without `mesh_relay_v1` (`sessionHasCapability(address, capMeshRelayV1)` in the announce sender). This gate operates independently of `mesh_routing_v1` — a peer must have both capabilities to participate in route exchange.

**Transit route invalidation for routing-only peers.** When the last total session of a routing-only peer (no relay-capable sessions were ever active) closes, `onPeerSessionClosed` calls `Table.InvalidateTransitRoutes(peerIdentity)` as a defense-in-depth measure. This method marks all transit routes (non-direct, hops < 16) where `NextHop == peerIdentity` as withdrawn (hops=16) without incrementing SeqNo (local-only invalidation — no wire withdrawal is generated). Direct routes are skipped because their lifecycle is governed by `RemoveDirectPeer`. When at least one route is invalidated, `announceLoop.TriggerUpdate()` fires an immediate announce cycle so neighbors learn the stale routes are withdrawn without waiting for the next periodic cycle (up to 30s). Under normal operation, the announce_routes relay gate prevents routes from routing-only peers from entering the table. `InvalidateTransitRoutes` guards against edge cases: routes learned before capability was fully negotiated, or stale entries from a previous relay-capable session of the same identity. The method returns the count of invalidated routes for logging.

**Disconnect withdrawal fan-out (parallel contract).** When a direct peer's last relay-capable session closes, `onPeerSessionClosed` in `node/routing_session.go` has to deliver the own-origin wire withdrawals returned by `RemoveDirectPeer.Withdrawals` to every remaining routing-capable peer **immediately** — the announce loop would otherwise delay withdrawal delivery up to one periodic cycle (30s). The fan-out is implemented by the dedicated helper `fanoutAnnounceRoutes(ctx, targets []routing.AnnounceTarget, withdrawals []routing.AnnounceEntry) (sent, dropped int)` in `node/routing_announce.go`. Its contract: (1) **one goroutine per target** — every peer is handled by its own goroutine so a single stuck inbound socket does not serialise delivery to the rest; (2) **per-peer bound = `syncFlushTimeout`** — inside each goroutine `SendAnnounceRoutes(ctx, …) → writeFrameToInbound(ctx, …) → sendFrameBytesViaNetworkSync(ctx, …) → networkBridge.SendFrameSync(ctx, …) → NetCore.SendRawSyncCtx(ctx, …)` threads the caller ctx end-to-end, and the write-path is bounded by the netcore sync-flush timeout (default 5s) regardless of the other peers; (3) **wall-clock bound = `syncFlushTimeout` of the slowest peer**, never `N × syncFlushTimeout` — this is the property that prevents peer-cleanup from starving the 1s metrics collector and the 2s `bootstrapLoop` under a writer-preferring `sync.RWMutex`; (4) **shared cancellation** — the fanout call site in `onPeerSessionClosed` passes `s.runCtx`, so shutdown short-circuits every goroutine at the top of its body and counts it as dropped; that same `ctx` is now observed *inside* the netcore sync send (via `SendRawSyncCtx`), so cancellation aborts an in-flight flush instead of silently waiting out the full 5s `syncFlushTimeout`; (5) **no shared state** — goroutines share only two `atomic.Int32` counters, no `Service` map is mutated; `SendAnnounceRoutes` takes its own short `s.mu.RLock` inside `writeFrameToInbound` and releases it before the blocking send; (6) **`crashlog.DeferRecover()` per goroutine** — one panicking target does not leave a WaitGroup hung or take down the cleanup goroutine; (7) **observability** — the outer `routing_direct_peer_removed` log line exposes `fanout_sent` and `fanout_dropped`, so a `sent ≪ dropped` divergence is a direct signal of stuck inbound sockets (e.g. NAT hairpin half-dead TCP) without stack traces. Regression coverage: `TestOnPeerSessionClosed_FanoutIsParallel`, `TestOnPeerSessionClosed_FanoutRespectsRunCtx`, `TestOnPeerSessionClosed_NoPanicOnZeroPeers`, `TestOnPeerSessionClosed_CollectsSentAndDroppedCounters` in `node/routing_integration_fanout_test.go`.

```mermaid
sequenceDiagram
    participant CL as Cleanup goroutine<br/>(onPeerSessionClosed)
    participant RT as routing.Table
    participant FO as fanoutAnnounceRoutes
    participant G1 as goroutine P1
    participant G2 as goroutine P2<br/>(stuck inbound)
    participant G3 as goroutine P3
    participant N as netcore

    CL->>RT: RemoveDirectPeer(identity)
    RT-->>CL: Withdrawals, ExposedBackups, TransitInvalidated
    CL->>CL: routingCapablePeers() under s.mu.RLock
    CL->>FO: fanoutAnnounceRoutes(runCtx, targets, withdrawals)
    FO->>G1: go SendAnnounceRoutes(ctx, P1, w)
    FO->>G2: go SendAnnounceRoutes(ctx, P2, w)
    FO->>G3: go SendAnnounceRoutes(ctx, P3, w)
    par P1 (fast)
        G1->>N: SendRawSyncCtx(ctx)
        N-->>G1: ok (~ms)
        G1->>FO: sent++
    and P2 (stuck inbound, bounded per-peer)
        G2->>N: SendRawSyncCtx(ctx)
        Note over G2,N: blocked up to syncFlushTimeout (5s)<br/>or until ctx cancel
        N-->>G2: ErrSendTimeout / ErrSendCtxCancelled → Close
        G2->>FO: dropped++
    and P3 (fast)
        G3->>N: SendRawSyncCtx(ctx)
        N-->>G3: ok (~ms)
        G3->>FO: sent++
    end
    FO-->>CL: sent=2, dropped=1 (wg.Wait completes)
    CL->>CL: log routing_direct_peer_removed<br/>fanout_sent=2, fanout_dropped=1
    CL->>CL: eventBus.Publish(TopicRouteTableChanged)
    CL->>CL: announceLoop.TriggerUpdate()
```
*Diagram — Parallel disconnect fan-out: one goroutine per target, wall-clock bounded by the slowest peer*

**TTL policy delegation.** Received announcements leave `ExpiresAt` as zero when constructing the `RouteEntry` in `handleAnnounceRoutes`. `Table.UpdateRoute` fills it using the table's own configured `clock` and `defaultTTL`. This ensures that all routes (direct via `AddDirectPeer`, learned via announcements) follow the same TTL policy, and tables created with `WithDefaultTTL` or `WithClock` behave consistently across all route sources.

### RPC observability

Three RPC commands expose routing table state for monitoring and debugging. They follow the same provider-injection pattern as metrics and chatlog commands — when `RoutingProvider` is nil, commands return 503 (unavailable) and are hidden from help output. Full field-level specification: [`rpc/routing.md`](rpc/routing.md).

**`fetch_route_table`** — Returns the full routing table snapshot. Each entry includes identity, origin, `next_hop` object (identity + live transport address/network), hops, seq_no, source (direct/hop_ack/announcement), withdrawn/expired flags, and remaining TTL. The `next_hop.address` and `next_hop.network` fields are live best-effort metadata queried after the snapshot — routing fields are atomic within `snapshot_at`. Response also includes total/active entry counts and snapshot timestamp. Sorted by (identity, origin, next_hop.identity, source).

**`fetch_route_summary`** — Returns a compact overview with `snapshot_at` timestamp, total/active/withdrawn entry counts, number of reachable identities, direct peer count, and the current flap detection state (per-peer withdrawal count, hold-down status). Flap state sorted by peer_identity. The `snapshot_at` field matches the same point-in-time semantics as `fetch_route_table` and `fetch_route_lookup`.

**`fetch_route_lookup <identity>`** — Returns selectable routes for a specific destination identity, ranked by the Phase 2 `CompositeScore` (hops + source-trust bonus + RTT bonus + health penalty — the same ranking the relay path applies via `routing.Table.Lookup`). Withdrawn / expired entries are filtered, and `HealthDead` transit claims are dropped; `RouteSourceDirect` claims are exempt from the Dead filter and scored with a `Bad`-equivalent penalty so they stay selectable as last resort. Deterministic tie-break: origin asc, next_hop asc, seq_no desc. Full contract in [`rpc/routing.md`](rpc/routing.md). Useful for diagnosing why a particular peer is or is not reachable via table routing.

The `RoutingProvider` interface (`rpc/provider.go`) has two methods: `RoutingSnapshot()` returns an immutable point-in-time copy of the routing table, and `PeerTransport(peerIdentity)` resolves a peer identity to its current transport address and network type. All three commands use `RoutingSnapshot()` for atomic data; `fetch_route_table` additionally calls `PeerTransport()` to enrich `next_hop` with live transport info. Console positional argument mapping is configured: `fetch_route_table` and `fetch_route_summary` take no arguments; `fetch_route_lookup` takes a single `<identity>` positional argument.

**Protocol version validation (outbound + inbound).** Both directions enforce `config.MinimumProtocolVersion`. On the *outbound* path, the welcome frame's version is checked; incompatible peers are rejected with log `outbound_peer_protocol_too_old`, receive a `peerScoreOldProtocol` (-50) score penalty, and an accumulating overlay-level ban score (`peerBanIncrementIncompatible` = 250) is applied via `penalizeOldProtocolPeer`. The ConnectionManager dial path (`openPeerSessionForCM`) returns an `incompatibleProtocolError` that carries confirmed version evidence (peer version, peer minimum, and client version from the welcome frame); `onCMDialFailed` extracts this evidence via `errors.As` and passes protocol versions to `penalizeOldProtocolPeer` so the reporter set and persisted lockout receive the same evidence as the legacy dial path. The client version string is pre-populated into `s.peerVersions[address]` before `penalizeOldProtocolPeer` runs, so `setVersionLockoutLocked` writes the complete `ObservedClientVersion` into the persisted lockout. The same pre-population is applied in the legacy outbound path and the inbound rejection path — on all three failure paths, the handshake frame is the only source of the client version since `learnIdentityFromWelcome`/`addPeerVersion` only runs on the success path. Without the structured error, the CM path would call `penalizeOldProtocolPeer(address, 0, 0)`, which correctly applies ban scoring but would skip the reporter set and lockout due to the zero-evidence guard. Verified by `TestCMDialFailed_PropagatesVersionEvidence`, `TestCMDialFailed_ZeroEvidence_NoReporterOrLockout`, and `TestCMDialFailed_PersistsObservedClientVersion`. A single failed attempt does not trigger a full ban — the overlay ban threshold is 1000, requiring 4 consecutive attempts before the peer is banned for `peerBanIncompatible` (24 hours). This graduated approach avoids permanently blacklisting peers that experience transient version mismatches during rolling upgrades. The version evidence (peer's protocol version and minimum version from the welcome frame) is recorded as a **version lockout** in `peerEntry.VersionLockout` and persisted to `peers.json`, but only when the evidence is confirmed — the remote `minimum_protocol_version` must exceed the local protocol version. Lockouts are bound to the peer's cryptographic identity when available; identity-less (address-only) lockouts expire after 7 days (`VersionLockoutMaxTTL`) to prevent stale entries from suppressing addresses reassigned to a different peer. Identity-bound lockouts persist until the local node's version changes (protocol version or client build increases), at which point stale lockouts are cleared automatically on startup via `clearStaleVersionLockoutsLocked`. A locked-out peer is skipped by `peerDialCandidates` regardless of lockout type. On the *inbound* path, the hello frame's version is checked; incompatible clients receive an error frame with `ErrCodeIncompatibleProtocol` and two independent ban tracks fire with distinct blast radii. The **per-peer storm-suppression signal** is emitted immediately: before the session is torn down, `emitPeerBannedNoticeByID(connID, now+peerBanIncompatible, PeerBannedReasonPeerBan)` delivers `connection_notice{code=peer-banned, reason=peer-ban}` on the still-open ConnID. The dialler-side gate 6c.2 routes the notice by `details.reason`: on `peer-ban` it calls `recordRemoteBanLocked` to suppress THIS specific `PeerAddress` only, leaving compatible siblings behind a shared egress (NAT gateway, VPN exit, Tor exit, multi-homed host) reachable. On `blacklisted` it suppresses the offending `PeerAddress` per-peer (`recordRemoteBanLocked`) AND accumulates the offender toward an IP-wide escalation (`noteRemoteIPOffenderLocked`); the IP-wide gate (`recordRemoteIPBanLocked`, which suppresses every sibling behind that egress including peers discovered later via peer exchange) engages ONLY once `ipWideRemoteBanMinOffenders` distinct, non-expired peers behind the same server IP have independently sent `blacklisted` — a single notice no longer undials innocent NAT/VPN/Tor-exit siblings. Either path keeps `cm_session_setup_failed` out of the ebus. The **transport-level IP blacklist** stays graduated as the safety net for sustained noise from the same IP: `addBanScore(connID, banIncrementIncompatibleVersion)` adds 250 to the remote IP's ban score per attempt, and only the 4th attempt crosses `banThreshold` = 1000, arming the 24-hour TCP-level block. When the threshold is crossed, `addBanScore` fires a second peer-banned notice with `reason=blacklisted` on the connection that tripped it, and from then on `handleConn` silently drops every TCP attempt from that IP until the window elapses. The inbound path also calls `penalizeOldProtocolPeer` for overlay-level ban scoring; the overlay per-peer track stays graduated (4 attempts to `peerBanThresholdIncompatible` = 1000), giving known peers a rolling-upgrade window on the persisted side. However, the reporter observation and version lockout are only recorded when the direction is confirmed — the remote peer's `minimum_protocol_version` must exceed the local protocol version (see "Direction invariant" below). Each confirmed incompatible-version observation is recorded in the node-owned **VersionPolicyState**, which aggregates reports from distinct peer identities. When ≥3 distinct peers report incompatibility (or ≥2 peers advertise a higher `client_build`), the node signals `update_available` in its `AggregateStatus` — the Desktop UI reads this pre-computed flag rather than computing it locally. Ephemeral observations expire after 24 hours, reporters are deduplicated by `PeerIdentity` (empty-identity observations are ignored). Active persisted lockouts also contribute to `update_available`, ensuring the signal remains true as long as any lockout exists — the UI does not lose the update hint while the node remains partially locked out. The version policy snapshot is recomputed twice within the confirmed-incompatible path in `penalizeOldProtocolPeer`: once by `recordIncompatibleObservationLocked` (before the lockout exists) and once after `setVersionLockoutLocked` (so the lockout's contribution to `update_available` is immediately visible). Without the second recompute, the snapshot would remain stale until the next unrelated event. Verified by `TestPenalize_SnapshotIncludesLockoutSignal`.

**Remote-ban recovery (two-scope).** `connection_notice{code=peer-banned}` is routed by `details.reason`. `peer-ban` writes only the per-peer `persistedMeta[address].RemoteBannedUntil` via `recordRemoteBanLocked`. `blacklisted` writes the per-peer offender row too AND records the offender (with its expiry) in the in-memory `Service.remoteIPBanOffenders` set; the IP-wide `Service.remoteBannedIPs` entry is only written via `recordRemoteIPBanLocked` once at least `ipWideRemoteBanMinOffenders` distinct, non-expired offenders behind the same egress IP have accumulated — and the recorded IP-wide horizon is the `ipWideRemoteBanMinOffenders`-th largest live offender expiry (so the IP-wide ban lapses the moment fewer than that many offenders remain, never inheriting a single long-lived notice's window). The dial-gate check `isPeerRemoteBannedLocked` consults the per-peer table and the IP-wide table on every candidate, so a record in either scope is enough to suppress. `remoteIPBanOffenders` is volatile (never persisted): the count is a transient escalation signal, and a restored active IP-wide ban already carries its own `Until`. A successful outbound handshake (`recordOutboundAuthSuccess`) therefore needs to clear only the two scopes it can legitimately speak for, in a single `peerMu` write section (with `ipStateMu` nested for the IP-wide clear) followed by one `flushPeerState` so the recovery cannot be split by a crash or restart: (1) the handshaking peer's own per-peer `RemoteBannedUntil` via `clearRemoteBanLocked` — unconditional, because the handshake itself is direct proof this address is accepting us again; a no-op when no row exists; (2) the IP-wide `remoteBannedIPs` entry via `clearRemoteIPBanLocked`, keyed on the canonical host of the dialled endpoint, so every sibling `PeerAddress` behind that egress IP is freed by the dial gate together with the host that carried the recovery — one sibling's success speaks for all siblings because `reason=blacklisted` is an IP-scoped decision. Per-peer rows with `reason=peer-ban` on OTHER sibling addresses are intentionally NOT touched by the IP-wide recovery: those are standalone responder decisions scoped to specific addresses, and a handshake with one sibling is not proof the responder has forgiven a peer-specific ban on another address. Either clear returning `true` triggers the shared `flushPeerState` so the on-disk snapshot matches the in-memory state before the next dial cycle. Verified by `TestRecordOutboundAuthSuccess_ClearsRemoteIPBan`, `TestRecordOutboundAuthSuccess_ClearsRemoteIPBanFreesSiblings`, and `TestRecordOutboundAuthSuccess_PreservesPeerBanSiblingRows`.

**Counter ownership boundary.** Two independent counters track incompatible-version events and they are NOT projections of each other. `peerHealth.IncompatibleVersionAttempts` is the per-address cumulative counter used for overlay ban scoring — each attempt adds `peerBanIncrementIncompatible` to the overlay penalty, and the ban fires when the cumulative penalty reaches `peerBanThresholdIncompatible`. `versionPolicyState.incompatibleReporters` is the per-identity dedup set used for the `update_available` signal — it counts distinct peer identities that reported incompatibility within the 24-hour observation window. The per-address counter survives indefinitely (cleared only by operator `add_peer`); the per-identity set is subject to observation TTL expiry. Both are mutated by `penalizeOldProtocolPeer` under `peerMu` (the peer-domain lock), with `ipStateMu` / `statusMu` / `deliveryMu` nested only in the specific sub-sections that touch those domains; they serve different consumers (ban scoring vs update signal) and have different lifecycles.

**Safety invariant: no evidence, no lockout.** A persisted version lockout is set only when confirmed version evidence exists — the remote peer's `minimum_protocol_version` must strictly exceed the local `config.ProtocolVersion`. When version evidence is absent (peerMinimum = 0, e.g. from a bare error code without a welcome frame) or does not confirm incompatibility (peerMinimum ≤ local), no lockout is created. This prevents the connection manager from permanently suppressing a peer based on a transient or unverifiable error. The safety invariant is verified by negative tests (`TestVersionLockout_NoEvidenceNoLockout_ZeroMinimum`, `TestVersionLockout_NoEvidenceNoLockout_MinimumBelowLocal`) and a positive test (`TestVersionLockout_EvidenceConfirmed_LockoutSet`).

**Direction invariant: inbound old peers do not feed the upgrade signal.** The `update_available` signal and the `incompatibleReporters` dedup set must only reflect the "they think we're old" direction — i.e., when a remote peer's `minimum_protocol_version` exceeds our local `config.ProtocolVersion`. When we reject an old inbound peer because *their* version is below *our* minimum, this is the opposite direction: the remote peer is outdated, not us. `penalizeOldProtocolPeer` applies ban scoring (health counters, score penalty, ban escalation) unconditionally for both directions, but the `recordIncompatibleObservationLocked` call and the `setVersionLockoutLocked` call are guarded by `peerMinimum > config.ProtocolVersion`. Without this guard, a cluster of old peers connecting inbound could incorrectly trigger the "you need to upgrade" CTA for the local operator. The invariant is verified by `TestInboundOldPeer_DoesNotFeedReporterSet` (4 old inbound peers with peerMinimum ≤ local → reporter count stays 0, update_available stays false) and `TestInboundOldPeer_ZeroMinimum_NoReporter` (peerMinimum = 0 with known identity → reporter count stays 0).

**Multi-address lockout propagation.** Version incompatibility is a property of a peer's software version, not of a specific network endpoint. When `setVersionLockoutLocked` records a lockout for an address that has a known `PeerIdentity`, it propagates the same lockout to all other addresses in `persistedMeta` that map to the same identity via `peerIDs`. A sibling address receives the propagated lockout when it either has no active lockout, or the new evidence is stronger (higher `ObservedMinimumProtocolVersion`) or fresher (newer `LockedAt` timestamp). Without the refresh path, already-active sibling entries would keep stale data forever — skewing diagnostics like `ObservedClientVersion` and `MaxObservedPeerVersion` for any logic or UI that inspects the propagated lockout contents. This prevents `peerDialCandidates` from re-dialing the same incompatible peer via an alternative address — a futile attempt that wastes resources and risks remote-side ban escalation. Propagation only occurs for identity-bound lockouts; address-only lockouts (no known identity) cannot be safely propagated because two addresses without identity may belong to different peers. The existing per-address lockout check (`isPeerVersionLockedOutLocked`) remains the filter in `peerDialCandidates` — propagation ensures every address for the identity has a lockout entry, so the per-address check correctly suppresses all of them. Verified by `TestVersionLockout_PropagatedByIdentity` (3 addresses, 2 with same identity: lockout set on one propagates to the other, third address with different identity unaffected), `TestVersionLockout_NoIdentity_NoPropagation` (no identity: lockout does not propagate), `TestVersionLockout_SiblingRefreshedWithStrongerEvidence` (higher minimum refreshes already-active sibling), and `TestVersionLockout_SiblingRefreshedWithFresherTimestamp` (newer timestamp refreshes sibling's client version).

**Build signal lifecycle.** The `peerBuilds` and `peerVersions` maps are session-scoped: populated during the handshake (welcome/hello frame processing), and cleared on disconnect (`markPeerDisconnected`). This ensures that only currently connected peers contribute to the build-based update signal — a disconnected peer's stale build number does not keep `update_available` artificially raised. Entries are also deleted during stale-peer eviction. Reconnection repopulates both maps from the fresh handshake frame.

**Compatible observation lifecycle.** A successful handshake (`markPeerConnected`, called for both outbound and inbound connections) is definitive proof that the peer is compatible. It clears all stale incompatible-version evidence for that specific peer: `IncompatibleVersionAttempts` is reset to 0, `LastIncompatibleVersionAt` is zeroed, `ObservedPeerVersion` and `ObservedPeerMinimumVersion` are zeroed, the peer is removed from the `incompatibleReporters` dedup set, and any active persisted `VersionLockout` is cleared. Lockout clearing is identity-wide: because `setVersionLockoutLocked` propagates lockouts to all addresses sharing the same `PeerIdentity`, `markPeerConnected` mirrors this by clearing the lockout from all sibling addresses of the same identity — not just the address that completed the handshake. All failure-related health fields are reset via `resetPeerHealthForRecoveryLocked` — a single helper that is the sole source of truth for the recovery field set. The helper clears: error strings (`LastError`, `LastErrorCode`, `LastDisconnectCode`), version evidence (`IncompatibleVersionAttempts`, `LastIncompatibleVersionAt`, `ObservedPeerVersion`, `ObservedPeerMinimumVersion`), dial-suppression state (`ConsecutiveFailures`, `LastDisconnectedAt`, `BannedUntil`), and score repair (negative scores are floored to 0). The same helper is used for both the direct address and sibling addresses, along with the IP-wide ban for each sibling's IP (handled by the caller). Score flooring neutralises stale `peerScoreOldProtocol` penalties that would otherwise keep siblings eviction-eligible (`peerEvictScoreThreshold = -20`) even after the identity has proven compatibility; the floor is applied before any connect bonus so that peers with legitimately positive scores are not inflated. `LastError` is especially important because `rankPeerHealth` subtracts `len(health.LastError)` from the peer ranking score — a stale error string on a sibling address depresses its ranking even after the identity has proven compatibility. Without full field clearing, siblings would retain stale failure state that keeps them out of candidate selection (ban filtering, exponential cooldown), depresses peer ranking, and shows misleading diagnostics in `fetchPeerHealth`. Without identity-wide clearing, sibling addresses would remain locked out despite the peer having proven compatibility via another endpoint, continuing to suppress dial candidates and incorrectly contributing to the `update_available` signal. The version policy is then recomputed so the signal immediately reflects the reduced evidence. This is the counterpart to the incompatible observation flow — together they form a closed lifecycle: incompatible evidence accumulates via `penalizeOldProtocolPeer`, and compatible evidence clears it via `markPeerConnected`. The compatible observation only affects the connecting peer's identity — evidence for other identities is untouched. Verified by `TestCompatibleObservation_ClearsReporterAndLockout` (single peer: full cleanup after successful handshake), `TestCompatibleObservation_OnlyAffectsConnectingPeer` (one peer cleared, others retained), `TestCompatibleObservation_ClearsLockoutAcrossIdentity` (handshake on one address clears lockout for all addresses of the same identity, leaves other identities untouched), `TestCompatibleObservation_ClearsSiblingHealthDiagnostics` (handshake on one address resets per-address health diagnostics for sibling addresses of the same identity, leaves other identities untouched), and `TestCompatibleObservation_ClearsSiblingBansAndCooldowns` (handshake on one address resets ban/cooldown fields and IP-wide bans for all sibling addresses of the same identity across different IPs, leaves other identities untouched), and `TestCompatibleObservation_RepairsStaleScorePenalties` (handshake on one address floors negative scores to 0 for sibling addresses of the same identity, applies connect bonus to direct address, leaves other identities' scores unchanged).

**Ban clearing and operator override.** A successful handshake also clears `BannedUntil` and the IP-wide ban — if the peer completed the handshake, it is compatible. The `add_peer` RPC command is the explicit operator override mechanism: it clears `BannedUntil`, resets all incompatible-version diagnostics (`IncompatibleVersionAttempts`, `LastErrorCode`, `LastDisconnectCode`, observed version fields), removes any persisted version lockout (`VersionLockout`) from the peer's metadata, and removes the peer's identity from the `incompatibleReporters` dedup set. The override is fully identity-wide: if the target address has a known `PeerIdentity`, all sibling addresses sharing that identity also have their lockouts cleared and their health fully reset via `resetPeerHealthForRecoveryLocked` (error strings, version evidence, dial-suppression state, negative score floor to 0), along with the IP-wide ban for each sibling's IP. Score flooring on override mirrors the handshake path — it neutralises stale `peerScoreOldProtocol` penalties without inflating peers with positive scores, ensuring operator-overridden siblings are no longer eviction-eligible. This mirrors the identity-wide clearing in `markPeerConnected` and the identity-wide propagation in `setVersionLockoutLocked`. Without identity-wide clearing, a manual retry of one address would leave sibling addresses suppressed by stale cooldown and ban data even though the operator explicitly wanted the peer re-dialed, keeping it out of normal candidate selection on its other addresses. The version policy is recomputed immediately so neither the lockout nor the reporter contributes to `update_available`. If the peer is already known, `add_peer` moves it to the front of the dial queue and responds with "already known, moved to front" instead of adding a duplicate. Network announcements do not clear `BannedUntil`; third-party gossip may add a candidate address, but compatibility remains proven only by direct handshake. Verified by `TestAddPeer_ClearsVersionLockout` (single address), `TestAddPeer_ClearsLockoutAcrossIdentity` (identity-wide: 3 addresses, 2 sharing identity), `TestAddPeer_ClearsReporterForIdentity` (reporter count decremented), `TestAddPeerFrame_ClearsLockoutAcrossIdentity` (end-to-end: identity-wide lockout clearing, health diagnostic reset, and reporter removal through the actual command path), and `TestAddPeerFrame_ClearsSiblingBansAndCooldowns` (end-to-end: sibling ban/cooldown/IP-ban clearing across different IPs through the actual `addPeerFrame` handler, different identities untouched), and `TestAddPeerFrame_RepairsStaleScorePenalties` (end-to-end: negative score flooring and connect bonus through the actual `addPeerFrame` handler, sibling scores floored to 0, different identities' scores unchanged).

**Outbound reject visibility.** A rejected outbound dial — where the remote welcome frame indicates a version below `MinimumProtocolVersion` — is fully visible in the `PeerHealthFrame` snapshot even though no lasting connection or session exists. The mechanism: `penalizeOldProtocolPeer` calls `ensurePeerHealthLocked(address)` which creates or retrieves the `peerHealth` entry in the address-keyed `s.health` map. Health entries are not session-scoped — they persist in memory across failed dial attempts, surviving until the peer is evicted. After `penalizeOldProtocolPeer` returns, the health entry contains: `LastErrorCode` = `incompatible-protocol-version`, `LastError` = `"protocol version too old"`, `IncompatibleVersionAttempts` (incremented), `LastIncompatibleVersionAt` (timestamp), `ObservedPeerVersion` and `ObservedPeerMinimumVersion` (version evidence from the welcome frame), and `VersionLockoutActive` = true (when the lockout was set). `peerHealthFrames()` iterates all entries in `s.health` and emits a `PeerHealthFrame` row per address, regardless of connection state — disconnected and never-connected peers appear in the snapshot with all diagnostic fields populated. Additionally, `penalizeOldProtocolPeer` clears any previously stored `LastDisconnectCode` — a pre-handshake reject supersedes prior post-handshake disconnect codes so the snapshot is unambiguous. When the CM path carries `ClientVersion` from the welcome frame, `onCMDialFailed` pre-populates `peerVersions` so that `ClientVersion` also appears in the snapshot. The Desktop UI receives these fields via `fetch_peer_health` and can display the rejection reason, attempt count, and lockout status without any additional computation. The full operator-visible contract — including `LastErrorCode`, `LastDisconnectCode` (empty), `ClientVersion`, `ObservedPeerVersion`, `ObservedPeerMinimumVersion`, `VersionLockoutActive`, `Score`, `ConsecutiveFailures`, and `PeerID` — is verified end-to-end by `TestOutboundIncompatibleReject_HealthSnapshotComplete`, which calls `peerHealthFrames()` after an outbound incompatible reject and asserts every diagnostic field in the returned `PeerHealthFrame`.

**Diagnostic code lifecycle.** Two machine-readable error code fields serve different diagnostic purposes. `LastErrorCode` captures the protocol error from the most recent *pre-handshake* rejection (e.g. `incompatible-protocol-version`), set by `penalizeOldProtocolPeer`. It survives across failed reconnect attempts but is cleared on successful reconnect (`markPeerConnected`) and by operator override (`add_peer`). `LastDisconnectCode` captures the protocol error that caused the most recent *post-handshake* socket teardown (e.g. `frame-too-large`, `rate-limited`). It is empty when the disconnect was clean (err == nil), non-protocol (network timeout, EOF), or when the error maps to the generic `protocol-error` sentinel. It is cleared on clean disconnect, on successful reconnect, by operator override, and by `penalizeOldProtocolPeer` — a pre-handshake incompatible reject supersedes any prior post-handshake disconnect code because the latest failure event is a connection-level rejection, not a session teardown, and keeping both would create a mixed diagnostic snapshot that misrepresents the peer's current state. Both fields are exposed in `PeerHealthFrame` as `last_error_code` and `last_disconnect_code`. All recovery-path field resets (successful reconnect, operator override) are centralised in `resetPeerHealthForRecoveryLocked` to eliminate field-by-field duplication and prevent future drift when new health fields are added. Verified by `TestPenalizeOldProtocol_ClearsStaleLastDisconnectCode` (pre-handshake reject clears previously stored `LastDisconnectCode`, sets `LastErrorCode` and `LastError`).

**Version-detail refresh under lockout.** A locked-out peer is suppressed by `peerDialCandidates`, so the local node will not initiate outbound connections to it. This raises the question of how version evidence gets refreshed if the peer upgrades. The answer is the inbound path: a locked-out peer can still dial us. When an inbound connection arrives, the hello frame carries the peer's current protocol version and minimum version. If the peer is still incompatible, `penalizeOldProtocolPeer` updates the `peerHealth` observed-version fields and refreshes the lockout snapshot with fresh evidence. If the peer has upgraded and is now compatible, the inbound handshake succeeds — `markPeerConnected` triggers a compatible observation (see above), clearing the lockout, reporter evidence, and all incompatible-version diagnostics, and the peer re-enters normal rotation. When the *local* node upgrades, `clearStaleVersionLockoutsLocked` runs on startup and clears all lockouts whose `LockedAtLocalVersion` fingerprint no longer matches, re-enabling outbound dials to previously locked-out peers. The combination of inbound-path refresh and startup lockout clearing ensures that version lockouts are self-healing in both directions — remote upgrade and local upgrade — without requiring any explicit polling or probe mechanism.

**Build-based policy runtime scope.** The `peerBuilds` and `peerVersions` maps are intentionally session-scoped and not persisted to `peers.json`. The `max_observed_peer_build` field in `VersionPolicySnapshot` is a runtime-only diagnostic: it reflects the highest `client_build` seen from currently connected peers and resets to zero on node restart. This is correct behavior — stale build data from a previous session should not influence the `update_available` signal, because the build landscape may have changed (peers upgraded, new peers appeared). After restart, the build signal stays `false` until peers reconnect and complete handshake, at which point `peerBuilds` is repopulated from fresh hello/welcome frames. The persisted `VersionLockout` already captures the protocol-level incompatibility evidence that must survive restarts; build metadata is supplementary and does not require persistence.

**Restart durability scoping.** The `update_available` signal's durability across restarts is achieved exclusively through persisted version lockouts, not through the reporter dedup set. The `incompatibleReporters` set (which drives the "≥3 distinct peers" threshold) is runtime-only: it resets to zero on restart and repopulates only as peers reconnect and fail handshake. However, `recomputeVersionPolicyLocked` independently checks all persisted lockouts in `peers.json` — if any `VersionLockout.IsActiveAt(now)` returns true, the `lockoutSignal` fires and `update_available` remains `true` regardless of the reporter count. This means: before restart, the signal may come from reporters, lockouts, or both; after restart, it comes from lockouts alone until peers reconnect. The `incompatible_version_reporters` count may drop to zero after restart even though `update_available` is still `true` — the `update_reason` will be `incompatible_version_reporters` (lockout-sourced), not `peer_build_newer`. This is consistent: the reporter count is a supplementary diagnostic, not the sole source of truth for the signal. `MaxObservedPeerVersion` also survives restarts through the same lockout path — each active lockout contributes its `ObservedProtocolVersion` to the maximum.

**Version diagnostic persistence.** The machine-readable version diagnostic fields (`LastErrorCode`, `LastDisconnectCode`, `IncompatibleVersionAttempts`, `LastIncompatibleVersionAt`, `ObservedPeerVersion`, `ObservedPeerMinimumVersion`) are persisted alongside the legacy health fields (`ConsecutiveFailures`, `LastError`, `Score`, `BannedUntil`) in `peers.json` via `peerEntry`. `buildPeerEntriesLocked` copies all diagnostic fields from the in-memory `peerHealth` entry into the `peerEntry` during flush, and the restore path in `Service` initialization hydrates them back. Without this persistence, a restart would drop the operator-visible evidence that created the lockout — the lockout itself survives through `VersionLockout`, but `peerHealthFrames()` would return an empty diagnostic snapshot with no `LastErrorCode`, no attempt count, and no observed version evidence. Additionally, `peerHealthFrames()` sources `ClientVersion` from the session-scoped `peerVersions` map, which is empty on startup. The restore path seeds `peerVersions` from `VersionLockout.ObservedClientVersion` for each persisted peer with an active lockout, so the operator-visible client version string survives restarts without requiring a reconnection. When a peer reconnects successfully, the handshake repopulates `peerVersions` from the fresh welcome/hello frame, overriding the persisted value. The round-trip is verified by `TestVersionDiagnostics_PersistRoundTrip` (JSON marshal/unmarshal of `peerEntry` preserves all 6 diagnostic fields), `TestVersionDiagnostics_BuildAndRestoreRoundTrip` (full in-memory cycle: `penalizeOldProtocolPeer` → `buildPeerEntriesLocked` → marshal → unmarshal → hydrate → assert all fields match pre-flush state), `TestClientVersion_SurvivesRestartViaLockout` (CM path with `ClientVersion` → flush → restore → `peerVersions` repopulated from lockout), and `TestVersionDiagnostics_DiskRestartRoundTrip` (end-to-end on-disk restart: `penalizeOldProtocolPeer` → `flushPeerState()` → real `NewService` reload from `PeersStatePath` → `peerHealthFrames()` asserts all diagnostic fields including `LastErrorCode`, `LastDisconnectCode`, `IncompatibleVersionAttempts`, `ObservedPeerVersion`, `ObservedPeerMinimumVersion`, `ClientVersion`, `VersionLockoutActive`, `Score`, `ConsecutiveFailures`, and `LastError` survive the disk round-trip).

**Lockout-aware peer list trimming.** The persisted peer list is capped at `maxPersistedPeers` (500) entries to bound disk and memory footprint. `trimPeerEntries` sorts entries by score descending and truncates the tail. Without special handling, a peer with an active `VersionLockout` and a low score (e.g. `peerScoreOldProtocol = -50`) would fall into the trimmed tail. After restart the lockout entry disappears, the peer re-enters the dial candidate pool, the node wastes a connection attempt, and the incompatible-version evidence that justified the lockout is lost. To prevent this, `trimPeerEntries` partitions entries into two buckets before applying the top-N cutoff: entries with an active `VersionLockout` (`IsActive() == true`) are unconditionally retained, and the remaining budget (`maxPersistedPeers - len(locked)`) is filled from the highest-scored non-lockout entries. This mirrors the separate persistence bucket for IP-wide bans (`bannedIPs` section in `peerStateFile`) — both represent durable suppression decisions that must not be silently discarded by a size cap. In the extreme edge case where locked entries alone exceed the budget (500+ distinct incompatible peers), all locked entries are kept and no non-lockout entries are persisted — this is operationally implausible but handled for correctness. Verified by `TestTrimPeerEntries_ProtectsActiveLockouts` (locked peer with worst score survives trim while normal bad-score peer is dropped, total remains `maxPersistedPeers`) and `TestTrimPeerEntries_NoLockout_BehaviorUnchanged` (without lockouts, trim produces exactly `maxPersistedPeers` entries as before).

**Lockout-aware eviction.** The in-memory eviction sweep (`evictStalePeers`) runs every `peerEvictInterval` (10 min) and deletes peers whose score is at or below `peerEvictScoreThreshold` (-20) and whose last successful connection (or `AddedAt`, if never connected) exceeds `peerEvictStaleWindow` (24 h). Without a lockout guard, a confirmed incompatible peer — which accumulates `peerScoreOldProtocol = -50` and by definition never completes a handshake — would be evicted from `s.health`, `s.persistedMeta`, `s.peerVersions`, and `s.peerIDs` before the next `flushPeerState` writes to disk. This destroys the `VersionLockout` in memory, making the peer immediately dialable again and losing the incompatible-version evidence. The guard mirrors the existing bootstrap and connected-peer exemptions: before applying the score threshold, `evictStalePeers` checks whether `persistedMeta[address].VersionLockout.IsActive()` and skips eviction if true. Combined with the `trimPeerEntries` lockout-aware partitioning (which protects the on-disk path), this ensures a version lockout survives both the runtime eviction sweep and the persistence trim. Verified by `TestEvictStalePeers_ProtectsActiveVersionLockout` (locked peer with worst score and 48 h staleness survives eviction, normal peer with same score is evicted, lockout and health data remain intact).

**Periodic reporter TTL repair.** The version policy snapshot is recomputed on each observation event (compatible or incompatible) and on each operator override (`add_peer`). However, reporter observations that age past the 24-hour TTL are only expired during a recompute — if no new events arrive, stale reporters could keep `update_available` artificially raised. To bound the staleness window, `maybeRecomputeVersionPolicyPeriodic` runs as part of `refreshAggregateStatusLocked` on the 2-second `bootstrapLoop` ticker but is throttled to at most one actual recompute per 60 seconds (`versionPolicyRepairInterval`). This means expired reporters are cleaned within 60 seconds of crossing the 24-hour TTL boundary, regardless of event traffic. The 60-second cadence is a deliberate trade-off: well below the 24-hour TTL (making the repair lag negligible) while keeping overhead trivial on the fast ticker. The periodic path is a pure repair mechanism — it does not create `versionPolicyState` if none exists (no-op when there are no observations to expire). Verified by `TestPeriodicRepair_ExpiresStaleReporters`, `TestPeriodicRepair_ThrottledByInterval`, and `TestPeriodicRepair_NilPolicyIsNoOp`.

**`max_observed_peer_version` semantics.** This field reports the highest protocol version seen from peers that are incompatible with us. It is computed from two sources during `recomputeVersionPolicyLocked`: (1) the `PeerVersion` field from each runtime incompatible-reporter observation in the 24-hour dedup set, and (2) the `ObservedProtocolVersion` field from each active persisted lockout. The lockout scan walks all entries in `persistedMeta` without early exit, ensuring the true maximum is found regardless of map iteration order. The maximum across both sources is reported. After restart, only source (2) contributes until peers reconnect and repopulate the reporter set. The field is zero when no incompatible evidence exists (neither runtime observations nor persisted lockouts). It is informational — no business logic branches on this value; its consumer is the Desktop UI, which may display it alongside the update prompt to help operators understand how far behind the local version is. Verified by `TestMaxObservedPeerVersion_ScansAllActiveLockouts` (three active lockouts with different observed versions → maximum correctly reported).

**Identity-based routing resolution.** All inbound routing functions (`routingCapablePeers`, `resolveNextHopAddress`, `resolveRelayAddress`, `resolvePeerIdentity`) match on `connPeerHello.identity` (the Ed25519 fingerprint from the hello frame), not on `connPeerHello.address` (the listen address used for health tracking). This ensures NATed peers with non-routable listen addresses are correctly resolved as announce targets, relay destinations, and routing identities. The `countCapablePeers` diagnostic function also deduplicates by identity (`session.peerIdentity` for outbound, `info.identity` for inbound) — a single peer appearing via both an outbound session and an inbound connection is counted once.

**Connection ID.** Every TCP connection — both outbound (`peerSession.connID`) and inbound (`connPeerHello.connID`) — receives a monotonically increasing `connID` from `Service.connIDCounter`. The ID is exposed in `fetch_peer_health` via `PeerHealthFrame.ConnID` and propagated through the desktop layer (`PeerHealth.ConnID`, `ConsolePeerStatus.ConnID`). This allows UI and diagnostics to distinguish multiple sessions to the same peer address (e.g. an outbound session and an inbound connection from the same host, or rapid reconnect cycles). When multiple inbound connections exist for the same overlay address, `peerHealthFrames` emits a separate `PeerHealthFrame` row per connection, each with its own `ConnID`. If an outbound session also exists for that address, it is emitted as an additional row. This means `fetch_peer_health` may return more rows than the number of unique peer addresses.

**Per-connection inbound staleness.** Inbound connection eviction (`evictStaleInboundConns`) uses per-connection `lastActivity` timestamps instead of shared health state. Each `connPeerHello` tracks when the last frame was received on that specific TCP connection. A connection is evicted when `now - lastActivity >= heartbeatInterval + pongStallTimeout`. The per-connection approach prevents NATed peers that advertise the same listen address from being evicted due to an unrelated outbound session going stale — a scenario that occurred when multiple peers (or an outbound session to self) shared the same resolved health key. The same per-connection staleness check is applied in `connectedHostsLocked` to exclude zombie sockets from the connected hosts set.

**Periodic outbound fill.** The `ConnectionManager` event loop is event-driven: `fill()` runs on bootstrap completion, slot events (dial failed after max retries, active session lost), and hint events (`InboundClosed`, `NewPeersDiscovered`). Without a periodic trigger, the CM can get stuck at zero (or below target) outbound slots indefinitely if all candidates are in exponential cooldown at bootstrap — `fill()` returns 0 candidates, no slots are created, no slot events fire, and `fill()` is never called again. A periodic fill ticker (`periodicFillInterval = 30 s`) ensures newly-eligible peers (cooldown expired, ban expired) are picked up within 30 seconds regardless of event traffic. The ticker fires in the event loop `select` alongside slot/hint events, and is suppressed before bootstrap completes (peer list not yet loaded). The 30-second cadence is a deliberate trade-off: well below the shortest non-trivial cooldown (`peerCooldownBase = 30 s` for 1 failure) and the cooldown cap (`peerCooldownMax = 30 min`), so the retry lag after cooldown expiry is bounded at one tick. `fill()` itself is cheap when at capacity (early return on `freeSlots <= 0`) or when no candidates pass filtering. The interval is injectable via `ConnectionManagerConfig.FillInterval` for testing. Verified by `TestCM_PeriodicFill_RetriesAfterCooldownExpiry` (all peers in cooldown at bootstrap → 0 slots → cooldown cleared → periodic tick → slots created) and `TestCM_PeriodicFill_SuppressedBeforeBootstrap` (ticker fires but fill() is suppressed until bootstrap completes).

**Transit-aware relay receipt handling.** Both receipt ingress paths — `handleInboundRelayDeliveryReceipt` (raw inbound TCP) and the `relay_delivery_receipt` branch of `dispatchPeerSessionFrame` (authenticated outbound sessions) — apply the same three-path logic: (1) **local delivery** — the receipt's `Recipient` matches the node's identity or an active subscriber → stored via `storeDeliveryReceipt`; (2) **transit forwarding** — the `Recipient` is non-local but `relayStates.lookupReceiptForwardTo` returns a next hop for the message ID → the receipt is forwarded one hop back via `handleRelayReceipt` without local storage; (3) **gossip fallback** — neither local nor relay path exists, or `handleRelayReceipt` returns false (send failure / stale state) → the receipt is broadcast via `gossipReceipt` to routing targets so it can still reach the original sender through alternative paths. This gossip fallback is consistent with the contract documented in `handleRelayReceipt` ("caller is responsible for gossip fallback") and the pattern in `retryRelayDeliveries`. No ban scoring is applied at this handler level — neither for legitimate transit traffic nor for malformed frames (consistent with `push_delivery_receipt` and other receipt handlers where parse failures are silently dropped). Previously, the handler applied `banIncrementInvalidSig` (100 points) to any non-local receipt, which blacklisted compatible peers participating in multi-hop relay chains (10 forwarded receipts × 100 ban points = threshold 1000 → permanent ban). All four transit receipt tests assert `len(svc.bans) == 0` to prevent reintroduction of ban scoring. The session path is the primary production path — authenticated peers exchange all application frames through sessions, not raw TCP. Transit receipts are deduplicated via a two-layer scheme to prevent gossip fanout loops without permanently suppressing transiently unroutable receipts. Layer 1: `isTransitReceiptSeen` (read-only check at handler entry) drops receipts whose dedupe key (`recipient:messageID:status`) was already recorded by a prior successful delivery — prevents redundant relay/gossip processing. Layer 2: `markTransitReceiptSeen` (write) is called only AFTER confirmed delivery — after `handleRelayReceipt` returns true (relay chain forwarded), or inside `gossipTransitReceipt` after at least one `sendReceiptToPeer` succeeds. If no routing targets exist (node transiently isolated), or if targets exist but every `sendReceiptToPeer` fails (all sendCh channels full, all sessions stalled), the receipt is NOT marked — allowing a later arrival to retry after routes or send capacity recover. `gossipTransitReceipt` iterates targets synchronously (`sendReceiptToPeer` is an in-memory channel/queue write, not network I/O) and marks only on confirmed acceptance. Two simultaneous arrivals of the same receipt can both send before either marks, producing a one-time wire duplicate to each target — acceptable because the receiving node's `isTransitReceiptSeen` check will suppress re-processing, and the duplicate is self-limiting (not a loop). The shared `seenReceipts` key namespace (same format as `storeDeliveryReceipt`) ensures that a receipt seen via local delivery is also suppressed on the transit path and vice versa. Verified by `TestRelayDeliveryReceiptForwardsTransitReceipt` and `TestSessionRelayDeliveryReceiptForwardsTransitReceipt` (transit receipt forwarded via inbound and session paths respectively, ban map empty), `TestRelayDeliveryReceiptGossipsFallbackForUnrelatedRecipient` and `TestSessionRelayDeliveryReceiptGossipsFallbackForUnknown` (gossip fallback on both paths, ban map empty), `TestRelayDeliveryReceiptDedupeGossipInbound` and `TestSessionRelayDeliveryReceiptDedupeGossip` (duplicate transit receipt not re-gossiped), `TestRelayDeliveryReceiptDedupeForwardInbound` and `TestSessionRelayDeliveryReceiptDedupeForward` (duplicate transit receipt not re-forwarded), `TestRelayDeliveryReceiptRetryAfterNoTargetsInbound` and `TestSessionRelayDeliveryReceiptRetryAfterNoTargets` (receipt not marked on first no-target arrival, successfully gossiped on second arrival after route recovery), `TestRelayDeliveryReceiptRetryAfterAllSendsFail` (targets exist but all sendCh full and pending queues saturated, receipt not marked, retry succeeds after capacity recovers — tests the shared `gossipTransitReceipt` helper directly, covering both inbound and session paths; dispatcher wiring is independently pinned by the gossip fallback tests above).

**Target-aware aggregate network status.** The `computeAggregateStatusLocked` function derives the aggregate network status using the outbound peer target (`EffectiveMaxOutgoingPeers`, default 8) as the reference for healthy connectivity. The status thresholds are: **Offline** — no peers known; **Reconnecting** — peers known but none connected; **Limited** — 0 or 1 usable peer, or usable count below `target/2` (minimum viable connectivity); **Warning** — usable count at or above `target/2` but below `target`, or more than half of connected peers are stalled; **Healthy** — usable peers meet or exceed target. Previously, any 2 usable peers triggered Healthy regardless of target, which misled operators into believing the node had full connectivity with only 25% of intended peers. The target is read from `s.cfg.EffectiveMaxOutgoingPeers()` so nodes with custom `MaxOutgoingPeers` configuration get correct status reporting.

### RIB compaction (MaxNextHopsPerOrigin cap)

Post-Phase-1 the local routing table caps the number of LIVE `UplinkClaim` rows per Identity (rule 0 below excludes withdrawal tombstones from the count, so the bucket may briefly hold up to `K` live rows alongside one or more recently-withdrawn tombstones until `TickTTL` reclaims them). Without a cap, a node that learns the same destination from N distinct neighbours stores N entries — one per `Uplink` — and the table grows O(destinations × neighbours). On a 1000-node mesh that is hundreds of thousands of entries even before the time-derived overhead from the snapshot publisher and `fetchRouteTable` enrichment kicks in. The knob name (`MaxNextHopsPerOrigin`, env var `CORSA_MAX_NEXT_HOPS_PER_ORIGIN`) is kept for operator-facing stability — its semantic shifted from "per `(Identity, Origin)` bucket" pre-A1 to "per Identity bucket" post-A1, since `Origin` was dropped from the dedup key (see "Storage model" above).

**Configuration.** The cap is set via `routing.WithMaxNextHopsPerOrigin(n)` on the `Table` constructor and surfaces at the configuration layer as `config.Node.MaxNextHopsPerOrigin`, which the loader reads from `CORSA_MAX_NEXT_HOPS_PER_ORIGIN` and threads through to the Table option when the Service is constructed. Two distinct defaults apply at different layers: the bare `Table` constructor defaults to `0` (cap disabled) so unit tests and direct-Table callers observe the pre-cap behaviour deterministically, while the production-Service path through `config.Default()` defaults to `routing.DefaultMaxNextHopsPerOrigin = 4` (primary + 1 backup + 1 spare for probing + 1 inertial slot — see the constant's docstring for the trade-off rationale). Setting `CORSA_MAX_NEXT_HOPS_PER_ORIGIN=0` explicitly disables the cap as a first-class operator opt-out: every accepted entry is stored, the table grows unbounded, and the K-cap counters (`accepted`, `accepted_replaced`, `rejected_full`, `rejected_all_protected`) stay at zero. The other counters in the `cap_admission` JSON envelope (`seqno_flap_holdowns` from Phase 1 P2, `fast_invalidations` from Phase 1 P3, and `bad_hops_holdowns` from the bad-hops hysteresis layered on top of P3) are gated by INDEPENDENT knobs (`CORSA_MAX_SEQNO_ADVANCE_PER_WINDOW` / `CORSA_SEQNO_ADVANCE_WINDOW_SECONDS` / `CORSA_MAX_SANE_HOPS`; the hysteresis additionally honours the test/embedding opt-out `WithMaxBadHopsPerWindow(0)` and is transitively silenced together with P3 when `CORSA_MAX_SANE_HOPS=0`) — disabling the K-cap does NOT silence them, and disabling any P2/P3/hysteresis knob does NOT silence the K-cap counters. Each policy has its own kill switch; see the per-counter doc-comments on `RouteCapStats` and the `docs/rpc/routing.md` `cap_admission.*` field rows. The first rollout release shipped the config-layer default at `0` as well, so existing deployments observed pre-cap behaviour exactly during the soak period; the second release flipped the config-layer default to `4`, which the loader picks up automatically when the env var is unset.

**Admission policy** (in `ApplyUpdate` for the `idx<0` path — a `(Identity, Uplink)` pair that does not yet exist):

0. **Withdrawn rows are excluded from the cap counter and eviction search.** A row with `IsWithdrawn() == true` is a SeqNo-resurrection-guard metadata marker, not a routing slot — it neither competes with live entries for the K cap slots nor gets displaced to make room for one. Tombstones live "outside" the K-live slots; they are bound by `defaultTTL` rather than `K`, and are reclaimed by `TickTTL`. Rules 1–4 below operate on the LIVE (non-withdrawn) subset of the bucket.
1. If the per-Identity bucket holds fewer than the cap LIVE entries, append unconditionally → `AdmissionAccepted`.
2. Otherwise pick the worst evictable LIVE entry. The eviction key is **`(live > dead) → lowest TrustRank → highest Hops → earliest ExpiresAt`**, evaluated in this order — liveness is the FIRST tier, not a side-note. The "dead" tier here covers expired-but-not-withdrawn rows (`IsExpired(now) == true && !IsWithdrawn()`): a fully-live row dominates a stale-but-still-routing row regardless of trust, which keeps a bucket of K stale hop_acks from blocking a fresh announcement (without the liveness tier the dead hop_acks would dominate on trust alone and `Lookup`, which filters expired rows on the read side, would still return zero usable routes). Withdrawn rows are NOT in this comparison — they were excluded by rule 0. Direct (`RouteSourceDirect`) entries are NEVER evictable: a direct route corresponds to a live socket and only `RemoveDirectPeer` may release it. `RouteSourceLocal` claims are never persisted (synthetic self-route is injected at read time), so they don't appear in the eviction pool by construction.
3. If every live entry in the bucket is direct, the cap cannot evict anyone → `AdmissionRejectedAllProtected`, drop the new entry. The bucket is keyed by Identity and a direct route by construction has `Uplink == Identity`, so a single bucket can hold at most ONE direct entry (the row that represents the live session to that specific destination peer). Hitting this branch therefore means "the protected direct slot in this bucket is taken and a non-direct candidate for a different uplink arrived" — which in practice can only happen at very small K (most commonly K=1, where the bucket has exactly one slot and the direct row owns it) or in synthetic / test / direct-restore scenarios. Operators seeing a non-zero counter with K≥2 should investigate fixture state rather than fan-out sizing — direct routes do not stack across Identity buckets, so "cap smaller than direct-peer fan-out" is not the right framing.
4. Otherwise compare the incoming entry against the worst evictable candidate using the strict-better FLOOR key — **`(live > dead) → highest TrustRank → lowest Hops`**. The floor is intentionally narrower than the eviction key in step 2: `ExpiresAt` participates only in step 2 (picking the eviction victim, where the row closest to expiry is the cheapest to drop because TickTTL would reclaim it soon anyway) and is deliberately **NOT** in the floor. Every re-announce arrives with `ExpiresAt = now + defaultTTL`, strictly later than any existing entry's, so an `ExpiresAt`-inclusive floor would let any equal-trust + equal-hops re-announce displace a stable incumbent on each cycle and thrash the best-K winners (a 90%+ eviction rate symptom in dense meshes). The floor therefore admits only on a real improvement (live > expired, higher trust, or strictly fewer hops); equal-on-all-three means incumbent wins and the candidate is dropped → `AdmissionRejectedFull`. If the floor passes, overwrite the worst slot in place → `AdmissionAcceptedReplaced`.

The strict-better floor is what protects a stable best-K from churn: an attacker (or a flaky peer) flooding the bucket with progressively worse routes cannot displace the existing winners, and a benign re-announce flood with no real improvement leaves the bucket alone.

**Wire-format invariant.** The cap is a local ingestion policy that does NOT change the wire schema — `AnnounceProjectionFor` collapses storage down to (at most one live winner per `Identity` inline; tombstone suppressed when a live alternative exists — see "Live-vs-tombstone exclusivity on the wire" in Key invariants above) regardless of how many `Uplink` rows the table happened to keep, and the wire frame layout is identical with or without the cap. A mixed-version mesh (capped receiver, uncapped sender, or vice versa) therefore converges without any wire-level coordination: every node applies its own cap to the inbound stream and stores at most `K` LIVE (non-tombstone) entries per Identity. Withdrawal tombstones (`IsWithdrawn() == true`) bypass the cap and live alongside the K live slots; they are bound by `defaultTTL` (≈120 s) rather than `K`. This keeps the SeqNo resurrection guard intact: a delayed lower-SeqNo announcement for `(Identity, Uplink)` always finds the tombstone via the existing idx≥0 path in `ApplyUpdate` regardless of cap pressure.

The wire **content**, however, can differ between a capped and a cap-disabled node when the cap drops a row that `AnnounceProjectionFor` would have selected as the announce winner. The cap eviction key is `(live > dead) → trust → hops → expiry`, but `AnnounceProjectionFor`'s live-winner collapse picks min `Hops` within the live claims (Source is not part of the wire-winner ranking). With `K=1` and a bucket that holds one `hop_ack` at `Hops=7` plus one `announcement` at `Hops=2`, the cap retains the `hop_ack` (higher trust) and the node announces `Hops=7`; an uncapped node would have kept both rows and announced `Hops=2` (lower hops wins on the wire). This is the explicit trade-off the cap makes — confidence (trust tier) over hop count for routing decisions, mirroring `Lookup`'s ordering, even at the cost of announcing a longer-but-confirmed path. The wire frame is still well-formed and converges; receivers see at most one live entry per `Identity` from each capped neighbour, apply per-Identity cap admission to incoming entries, and merge them as usual.

**Multipath, hop_ack promotion and ExposedBackups.** All three behaviours that depend on multiple `Uplink` rows continue to work under the cap, because the cap retains up to `K` candidates per Identity rather than collapsing to a single best path:

- `Lookup(identity)` returns up to `K` candidates sorted by the existing trust+hops contract — the relay path's failover loop still has alternatives to try.
